B0002WYS6S|5.0|I know a lot of Dream Theater fans have sort of a love/hate relationship for the band. We all love the technical and musically complex side of Dream Theater, that create some of the greatest riffs and melodies in progressive music, yet we also hate Dream Theater's tendency to perform average cover songs, especially live, and of course, LaBrie's sometimes not so pleasant vocals. Well fear not, this performance consists of no cover songs, all the songs are excellent picks, we don't have any songs of their first album and only one song off of Falling into Infinity, which happens to be the best song off the album. Plus LaBrie, while not at his best here, at least does a good job putting energy into the performance, and there are a good chunk of instrumental songs and passages, I'd say LaBrie's only present for roughly half the performance.<br /><br /> We get some awesome songs from Images and Words like Pull Me Under, Awake, and a few songs from Falling Into Infinity and Scenes from a Memory. Although a majority of this album consists of material from Six Degrees of Inner Turbulence and Train of Thought, which was their new album at the time of this performance. Songs like In the Name of God and Stream of Consciousnesses just have so much energy put forth. Dream Theater does a good job expanding their solos and adding new elements when playing their music live so it sounds different than the studio. In fact, aside from LaBrie's vocals, most of the songs performed here are more enjoyable than their studio versions due to the new edge thats given to the songs. Petrucci really works some incredible solos and scales, Rudess is great on keyboards as usual with a rotating keyboard stand, Portnoy's drumming is on fire, and Myung's bass is incredible. My only real complaint is LaBrie's vocals sound worse here than in the studio, but it is nice seeing him put energy and passion in his voice, despite it not being at its best. I guess a minor complaint is I would have liked to have seen more songs off Images and Words and Scenes like Metropolis Part I, Learning to Live, Fatal Tragedy, or Overture 1928, but I the songs they did perform were still pretty excellent choices, and they definitely put forth an essential performance. Its really one of the few moments live that band really stands out at their best. And its 3 hours long, so we are getting an entire performance of excellent long jams and prog masterpieces. The second CD is kind of a making of documentary which sort of follows the band members around and they talk a little about the tour and how they prepare for it, as well as segments where Petrucci, Portnoy, and Rudess explain their equipment and setup for the concert, and a pretty fun Portnoy drum solo where he gets his fans to interact. This second disc is okay and really just a bonus, but its nice to see at least once. But overall, this is an essential performance and must see for any Dream Theater fan.

(NANO) GRAMS are entirely different from them.<br /><br />For those that are familar with the popular puzzles of Sudoku; they may recognize another Japanese puzzle known as, Nanograms. Nonograms are also known by several other names, such as, Japanese Paint by Numbers, Pixel Puzzles, Japanese Crosswords, Griddlers and numerous other names. They've also been called Paint by Sudoku and Binary Coloring Books but, those names do not describe the puzzles correctly.<br /><br />With Nanograms, you have a puzzle in which cells in a square grid need to be colored or left blank in accordance to the numbers given on the side of the grid. Solving the puzzle reveals a hidden picture. Nanograms are often black and white but, in this app, the number clues are colored to reveal the color of the squares. Personally. I feel having color in a Nanogram puzzle makes things much more challenging and, CONFUSING at times! For example: Having two different colored numbers may or may not have a space in between them. And lets say you had a black four followed by a red two. Thid could mean four black spaces, some empty spaces, and two red spaces, or...... it could simply mean that four black spaces followed immediately by two red ones. ??? This may be why many people, including myself, become so engaged with these puzzles. Many find Nanograms to be quite addictive. I think I will look around for a better interface for my records but for someone who just wants to hook up a mic or a cd player (or tape or whatever) this does work, it's just not what I would call \\"professional grade\\" equipment. 030758836X|5.0|THERE ARE NO SPOILERS WITHlN!<br /><br />Why are you reading this?<br /><br />If you had any sense, you'd be running to the store to buy this book...or better yet, just click your mouse a couple of times and have it whisked to you via Amazon.  Whatever gets it to you the quickest.<br /><br />Because you should absolutely be reading this book right now.  I used to think Ruth Rendell was the craziest murder thriller writer out there...the one who didn't just follow the footprints of the killer and put you in the room with him, but got you right inside his (or her) mind.<br /><br />And now I must add Gillian Flynn.<br /><br />This is more than just a terrific read, it's an absolute thrill ride; told from the point of view of both Nick and Amy, husband and wife, this is the story of a marriage gone foul.  Horrendously foul.<br /><br />It all begins when Amy goes missing, suddenly, a couple of hours after making breakfast for her husband on the morning of their fifth anniversary.  He gets the call at the bar he and his twin sister co-own, and when he returns home he finds his once comfortable house is now a crime scene awash with blood and overturned furniture.  And when the police get involved, the case of a missing wife turns extremely ugly.<br /><br />Because if Nick's innocent, why does every piece of evidence point to him? And WHY is he saying and doing such ridiculously incriminating things?<br /><br />There is scarcely any down time in this novel: Nick gets a call that the door to his house is wide open on page 22, and from that moment on he is spiraling down a greased chute of suspicion, guilt, and recrimination.  Since Nick's and Amy's sides of the tale are told in alternating chapters, you might wonder how the reader is kept in suspense, but Amy's side of the story is told from the point of view of her diary, and it is particularly through her entries that we get to discover a side of Nick that doesn't look good at all.<br /><br />And there's more, so much more: as the police get involved and the evidence at the house points to a staged fight, as one might guess all signs point to Nick.  But as Nick tells his side of the story and we begin to learn more about him, he and the case of his missing wife begin to peel away like metaphorical layers of an onion, and what we discover about their marriage, and about them, grows increasingly dark (more about this dark side later).<br /><br />Because Nick is under pressure now: under the white-hot glare of not only the police investigation, but also the public outcry led by a vengeful celebrity talk-show host, Nick's foundations are rooted out and torn from him.  Even his twin sister---the person closest to him his whole life--begins to see him as a foreigner, alien to her.  It is all Nick can do to even exist.<br /><br />And that's when things start turning seriously sinister.<br /><br />Let me tell you this: I'm a clever guy, and I'm always trying to think ahead of the story I'm reading, but there are surprises in this story that rocked me.  I mean, there are places in this novel where no force of nature or man could have torn me away from my reading...I'm not kidding.  It's that compulsive a tale.  My biggest problem lay in not being able to tell someone what I was reading--I just had to talk about this story.<br /><br />So, without telling you a thing about the plot other than what you'd read on the book flap--I sit here in my den, typing on my processor, imploring you to read this tale.  EVEN WHEN IT PLAYS ITS HAND, even when it lays its cards in front of you and shows you how it has played you, this novel still has surprises in store.<br /><br />For that's really where the true secret of \\"Gone Girl\\" lies: not in the footsteps we follow to find out what happened, but in the mind of its perpetrator.  It's a tale that thrills in the fraying of nerves as it takes its twists and turns--especially when you find out it's been playing with your mind all along.<br /><br />In regards tothose reviews of one- and two-stars, I will add one piece of advice: yes, the ending IS dark.  This mystery is not going to wrap up everything in one happy little ball--understand that.  But really, I would have been disappointed if it had.<br /><br />I loved \\"Gone Girl,\\" and I'm on my way to get \\"Sharp Objects\\" and \\"Dark Places,\\" her two previous works.  Flynn has decidedly earned my respect.<br /><br />Now--stop reading and BUY IT!!those reviews of one- and two-stars, I will add one piece of advice: yes, the ending IS dark. Even things that aren't really explained very well just add to the tension.<br /><br />For example, the hatred of the Burke Dennings character (who plays the movie director) for Ellen Burstyn's old butler, Karl, who he accuses of being a Nazi, just isn't explained at all in the movie but it does add to the overall sinister ambience of the whole movie. The only connection is Karl has a Germanic accent, but he claims to be Swiss. Dennings is the first person killed off by the demon but we know he's a bad guy because at the dinner party he gets drunk and acts boorishly, continually taunts Karl while he is trying to serve the guests, and at one point says he just found a public hair in his soup. Obviously, the demon has to kill this guy first. :-)<br /><br />In fact, almost no one in the film is having a very happy life. The film within a film takes place against the tense backdrop of the Vietnam War protests on the Georgetown University campus. Father Damien is having a crisis of faith, and is also worried about his aged mother who insists on living in her old house despite the dilapidated neighborhood now being unsafe. When in desperation Burstyn takes her daughter to a psychiatrist, the hapless shrink makes the mistake of commanding the demon to show himself while Regan is hypnotized. Cobb, who is intrigued by the murder mystery of the dead director and is delighted to be able to get Burstyn's (who plays a famous actress) autograph.<br /><br />Then there's the use of almost subliminal frames in the movie in the scene where father Damien is dreaming of his recently deceased mother. Just for a split second a cadaverous face appears on the screen which I presumed to be the dead mother but in researching this further (in the Wikipedia article on the movie) it is actually supposed to be an image of the Assyrian demon Pazuzu who father Merrin has battled before.<br /><br />Anyway, the whole audience shrieked at that point in the theater I was in, and at other scary moments such as Regan turning her head all the way around, rising off the bed, etc., the young woman seated in back of me was so terrified that she grabbed me from behind and held on to me. I never did turn around to see who it was, but maybe she came with a girlfriend instead of her boyfriend, but anyway, the whole movie theater was screaming in fear at the scariest moments so it was quite an experience. This was the movie's first week and I suppose the word hadn't gotten out yet about how disturbing it actually was. I went to it because a friend of mine said, oh, it's about a priest and it has to do with religion and it's supposed to be scary. Nothing else. Yeah right. So off we went. Needless to say, we were totally unprepared for what we saw. My friend was so freaked out he refused to go home that night and spent the next three days sleeping on my couch.<br /><br />The dramatic build up to the final climactic scenes are so well known I won't reprise them here, but it goes to show you that just when you thought the movie couldn't get any scarier the movie hits you over the head with another shocker of a scene. B004EPYZP8|5.0|Walking into `The Lincoln Lawyer', I was well aware that the film itself would have to work extra hard to earn my respect.  I don't really care much for courtroom dramas, and I find Matthew McConaughey to be a really dull presence on screen.  I know that everyone swoons over him and talks about his `Southern Charm', but I just don't buy it.  On top of that, I just don't find him to be a convincing actor.  I don't see layers in his work; I merely see a lazy approach to telling the same character over and over.  There has been a lot of talk of the sudden upswing in his career, starting with this particular film and moving right on into his string of successful turns this year.  Much has been made of his Oscar chances for `Magic Mike' and he's getting career best notices for the savage drama `Killer Joe'.  Needless to say, I didn't care much.  I have yet to see any of his films from this year and I wasn't really anticipating them much to tell you the truth.  When I hear the name Matthew McConaughey I don't expect much.<br /><br />So, last night I settled in to catch up on my DVR recordings and I noticed that I had `The Lincoln Lawyer' on my DVR.  I didn't record it, must have been the wife, but I wasn't really in the mood to think and so I decided to give it a whirl, since I expected it to be `good' at best and hardly something I'd feel compelled to dissect.<br /><br />I have to learn to stop judging every book by its cover.<br /><br />As a film, `The Lincoln Lawyer' is riveting and kept me glued throughout.  I was stunned.  The plot twists are engaging, and while the obviousness of the `villain' is a little too on the nose from the start, it becomes an easily forgivable offence since they aren't really interested in keeping his innocence ambiguous for very long.  The progression of the plot is well done and expertly paced by director Brad Furman, who holds onto the tension efficiently.  The final courtroom scene is pulse-racing for sure, and basically exemplifies everything that a courtroom drama should be.  In fact, I loved the fact that I never knew what Mick's next move would be and why he was making it, but when it all fell into place it made perfect sense.<br /><br />And that brings me to Mick, or should I say, Matthew McConaughey.  Why do I call this a case of Oscar snubbery?  I don't know if I'd personally nominate him, considering that last year was ridiculously strong, but a very solid case can be made for McConaughey being a snub because this role is every bit a perfect one to garner a star their first Oscar nomination.  What McConaughey does here is actually quite remarkable, and I don't say there merely because he took me by surprise or because I expected him to fail.  Any other actor in the role doing what he does would have garnered the same response from me.  I am not one to shy away from eating crow, and that is what I must do here.<br /><br />Something that I have always accused Matthew of doing is giving us a surface performance, with no actual depth.  I understand that in the beginning of his career he tried the whole `soul searching drama' stuff, and he did do `A Time to Kill' and yet I didn't care much for what he did in that film and so it doesn't count for me.  I've never felt that he has ever gotten under the skin of the character much at all; until now.  As Mick Haller, McConaughey layers so much on this man it's ridiculous.  In fact, the more I think about it the more stunned I am by the scope of his performance.  Mick is suave and under control and possesses that `Southern Charm' and sheer confidence that makes him a firm presence in any room.  That is something that McConaughey should be able to handle in his sleep.  But it is the tiny layers of panic that ride through his face when no one is looking that took me by surprise.  Mick wasn't just a stone, and as this `case' took over his personal life and threatened him in the severest of ways, you saw those cracks.  McConaughey let us in to see the inner turmoil, but then batted an eyelash and firmed up his stance and delivered a `performance' for the rest of the room that read otherwise.  Mick is a showman, and McConaughey mastered that aspect of his persona with ease.  The way he plays his part in the courtroom or the way he betrays his own panic by laying a threat of confidence out on his `client' is compelling and 100% believable.  The anger that floods his motives is also stirring and he sells his biggest moments with such authenticity.  I appreciated that he never overacted here, and there was place for it.  Instead, he displayed an honest depiction of emotional outbursts that stayed true to the man he was portraying.<br /><br />When all is said and done, Mick Haller may be human, but he is one cool cat.<br /><br />So, I give this very high marks.  The film is intriguing and engrossing from start to finish.  It has the ability to veer into `television courtroom drama' territory, as do a lot of films in this genre, and yet it pulls back with a glossy treatment and a plot that thickens rather well.  It's more `CSI: Miami' than `Law & Order', but Matthew McConaughey delivers a star performance that puts David Caruso to shame.r turmoil, but then batted an eyelash and firmed up his stance and delivered a `performance' for the rest of the room that read otherwise. B00000J7J7|5.0|I'm not the first to ask - with respect to awareness of musical talent -- \\"where have I been all these years?\\"  But, where have I been?  A faithful collector of Jarrett, Ibrahim, Monk, Evans, Corea and so many others, I find their unique synthesis in this splendid album by Brad Mehldau, recorded over 12 years back in rich, ambient sound.<br /><br />If you enjoy any of the pianist/composers cited, you should simply order this gorgeous work. More thoughtfully, this disc reminds me of Jarrett's \\"Bremen-Lausanne Concerts\\" at their best, and without the vocalise, and on Corea's wonderful \\"Children's Games,\\" without some of the dissonance or tension.<br /><br />Those who love piano jazz often try to infuse their reviews with supporting comments from authoritative sources to support recommendations. I cannot do that, no more than I could have with \\"Children's Games.\\"<br /><br />This is an uncommonly well-composed, improvised and recorded disc, running about 55, which embraces the elegiac form. It is largely in a minor key, and reflects both the depths and resurgence, programaticallyy, this music has throughout history.<br /><br />I have no idea why it's been a sleeper.  I can only say that at this price, this exceptional disc belongs in the collections of any humane listener who understands, or wants to comprehend, the \\"literature\\" of loss.  It is not at all gloomy, although you will find some dissonance and minors.  If anything, it is a musical performance that reaffirms both our ability to understand loss and build from it.<br /><br />Music, along with other creative anchors, is pivotal for anyone who hopes to grow or rebound from empty space.<br /><br />Mehldau fills the space gracefully and beautifully.  10/10.

B005LAIGOQ|5.0|Ghost stories are quite popular in England, probably because the weather and the winters give an optimal spooky atmosphere. The Woman in Black is just such a ghost story. It was first a novel, then made into a TV movie, a stage play, and two radio dramas before the release of this cinema version (which I saw on DVD).<br /><br />The story follows Arthur Kipps (played by Daniel Radcliffe), a young, widowed lawyer who has a four-year old son. He is still troubled by the loss of his wife. That has affected his work. The law firm sends him off to the North to settle the estate of Alice Drablow. She lived in Eel Marsh House at the end of Nine Lives Causeway. The causeway is only navigable at low tide. The house is full of paperwork that needs sorting to ensure the law firm's copy of her will is indeed the current one. This is Kipps last chance to prove he is serious about working for the firm. If he does not do well, he will be fired.<br /><br />He takes a train up to the town of Crynith Gifford. He meets a local man, Samuel Daily, who is quite rich (he owns the only car for miles and miles) and is friendly enough. The rest of the town is not so friendly because of the house's bad history and the local legend of the Woman in Black. She is mostly seen either by the house or on the causeway, but occasionally in town. Whenever she is seen, soon thereafter a child dies. Typically the death is a quite horrible one. Naturally the townfolk don't want anyone around the house. Kipps insists on going (to keep his job). As he researches through the papers, he uncovers information about the Woman in Black. Oh yeah, and strange noises and apparitions start happening too. And his son is supposed to come up on the weekend for a holiday!<br /><br />The movie centers on Kipps. It follows his investigation of the paperwork just enough to reveal the identity and motives of the Woman in Black. His character is well developed. His motives for staying are sensible enough and he is likeable enough that the viewer actually cares what happens to him. Developing an intelligent, sympathetic main character is critical in this type of movie. Radcliffe gives a great performance, subtle and nuanced without hysterics. He makes it more chilling.<br /><br />Much of the film shows him wandering through the house trying to look around corners for what just made that noise or what he just saw out of the corner of his eye. Plenty of cobwebs, dark corners, and creepy children's toys are made even more ominous by the low-key but highly evocative score. The atmosphere is great and delivers plenty of goosebumps. The story is interesting enough and the various twists are only occasionally predictable and always effective.<br /><br />The Woman in Black is a classic example of a haunted house movie, ranking with the best in that genre (The Uninvited, The Haunting, and The Changeling being the best).

038572179X|4.0|Like many other McEwan's books, \\"Atonement\\" slowly tells a gripping story and creates tension with an implicit promise to relieve it at the end. The little sneak previews along the way help us keep turning pages. In \\"Saturday\\", when Perowne sees the two figures on the square sitting with their backs to him we know that they do not bode well. In \\"Atonement\\", when Lola shows her bruised wrists to Briony, her explanation that it was the result of the Chinese burns inflicted by the cruel twins clearly will not do. McEwan never disappoints and by the end of the book our hunches are confirmed and the mysteries officially resolved. If reading Pynchon's novels feels like white water rafting at night reading McEwan is like following signs to a museum in a foreign city.<br /><br />The three parts of the book, like the three layers of the cake, are very different and have their distinct flavors. The first, signature McEwan (comfortable life and internal disquietude), the third sparkling, O'Henry-style giving the whole book a twist, the second, a story on its own, a bit incongruous between the other two, rice pudding between layers of chocolate truffle: if extending Robbie's life in fiction was part of Briony's atonement, the love and squalor of the narrative do not seem to weigh enough in the balance.<br /><br />There is often something savagely dark in McEwan's novels. His characters are made to deal with the beast in themselves and others. And his readers are made to share this preoccupation with them.

B0064NTZQ2|5.0|`Drive' really surprised me. I was never really interested in seeing this, I'd read the articles and seen the posters and ended up dismissing it, I only watched it because there wasn't anything else and didn't have high expectations. But, as it turns out, the film really blew my first impressions away. Instead of being bored, I found myself drawn into the film and caring for its characters, the acting was spot-on and Refn's direction made this movie a real gem.<br /><br />Ryan Gosling is Driver, a solitary guy who works as a mechanic and stunt car driver by day and getaway driver-for-hire at night, that is until he meets his neighbour, Irene (Carey Mulligan), and her son. The more time he spends with them, he falls for Irene in a somewhat romantic fairy tale....until it all comes crashing down when Irene's ex-con husband (Oscar Isaac) returns, bringing with him trouble and compelling Driver to protect Irene and her son from gangsters.<br /><br />What really stops this movie from becoming a cookie-cutter action-drama film is the fact that director Nicolas Winding Refn concentrates on giving us well-rounded characters. It's the characters that drive `Drive', not the action or the story and Refn's stylistic way of shooting it - with slow-motion and lingering shots of characters and still camera work - makes sure that what's unspoken speaks the loudest as he leaves it up to you to interpret their feelings and thoughts. It's not what happens when they speak, but more what goes unspoken through looks and body language. I don't know what you call it - noir or artistic or whatever - it's an approach that's been used by many directors before, but it makes `Drive' absorbing to watch.<br /><br />Of course, it wouldn't work without the actors giving top-notch performances and nobody lets the movie down here. Gosling is magnetic as Driver, conveying layers of emotion without uttering a word in some scenes. Albert Brooks and Ron Perlman also give stellar performances that make them more than one-dimensional villains. The one surprise for me was Bryan Cranston, I don't watch `Breaking Bad', but he proves he can stretch his acting chops far beyond quirky sit-com comedy characters. Carey Mulligan, Oscar Isaac, the criminally underused Christina Hendricks, all did admirably and were very believable in their roles.<br /><br />There are scenes of action throughout the film as well and they can be shockingly brutal, especially when compared to the mostly quiet tone of the film, one particular character's demise from a shot gun through a window will stick with me for a while and the car chases are tight and well executed.<br /><br />But this isn't an action film first, if I had to call it something, I'd say it's more like an artsy, character-driven drama. Whether you'll enjoy it or not probably depends on whether you think long, quiet, reflective scenes with lingering shots of characters staring at each other are thought-provoking and beautiful, or a director being self-indulgent and pretentious. Personally, I loved it because it engaged me emotionally and the cinematography work was stunning, this is one that stayed with me long after the credits rolled.

Unfortunately, the results fall short. Very short. My hope and expectations were something similar to the recent Jethro Tull \\"Aqualung\\" remix, which was amazing. Unfortunately, based on my intrepretation of the liner notes, it appears Ezrin was somewhat limited in what he could do, as he was working off a 16 track master in which several sounds were combined on each track. Accordingly, he was limited in his ability to actually remix many aspects of the album (for example, the drums). But the differences do not come across as being based on a new artistic vision, but rather the need to simply \\"change\\" something to create this product. It's rushed, let's be honest. I can accept Katniss is changed because of subsequent Hunger Games and war. However, nothing is tied up properly: it's just sloppy. For example, Finnick dies in one line. What is the point in building up a character that will do everything to save Annie, bring Peeta back to life in the arena, fights his own depression to continue saving Annie in order for him to die in one line. It's poor writing from an author that kept me hooked for two and a half books.<br /><br />Also, everything happens while Katniss is asleep, drugged, depressed, hiding or moaning. The whole point of a first person narrative is that you get to experience the action along with the character not so that you get to read the newspaper with her. If I wanted to just listen to depressing news, I'd turn on the TV. Again, this is just sloppy writing from an author who I expected much more from. It is almost as if she got told, \\"we want your book in 2010 rather than 2011 so can you make the ending come quick?\\" and rather than telling them where to go so she could keep her integrity, she accepted a bonus and ended it quickly. I hope this is what happened, because if she chose of her own accord to end it this way while not tying up the loose ends properly then she became a very poor writer overnight, which is clear from books one and two she isn't.<br /><br />I'll end my rant on this; I have given three stars purely on the basis that the trilogyis enjoyable. It is worth reading and if you get halfway through 'Mockingjay', you just can't stop: it'd be pointless. Collins still could've given us the ending she wanted, I just wish she had done it properly.

I'm serious, this particular track doesn't sound like it was mixed by a professional. It's awful. I'll concede that there's a better clarity in the mix, and it's certainly LOUDER. 1401235166|5.0|**This review contains spoilers**<br /><br />I found the seventeenth volume of the Fables series to be a fascinating entry to the series. I loved all of the new plot lines that have come up in the trade. The cubs inheriting the North Wind's Kingship was an interesting storyline, as is the prophecy Ozma told little Ambrose. One thing that bothered me was not hearing anything about Ghost regarding the prophecy or the contest for the heir to the North Wind's title. I know he's a secret to everyone but Snow, Bigby, and his siblings but surely THEY would have mentioned him, and how does he feel about all of this? We know what all of his siblings think but not Ghost yet. I'm not naive enough to think that it won't come up later in this storyline BUT it'd be nice to hear him get acknowledged in this Cub-centric arc.<br /><br />The storyline with Bufkin in Oz is also really fun, he and his crew of friends grew on me fast. I was afraid at first that I'd think of Bufkin's adventures as the B-plot but it actually managed to keep my attention just as much as the other stories.<br /><br />I'm really enjoying the parts with Mrs. Spratt shedding her old image (and a lot of weight) and wanting revenge on the Fables she grew to resent is an inspired idea. I cannot say how much I love that new plotline! I always just thought of Mrs. Spratt as being a background character who was only as hateful as she was because she was just a miserable person (or the fact that she was a widow after Ghost accidentally killed her husband) but seeing what she had to go through being one of the few unattractive fable women among plenty of gorgeous ones who are more well known and regarded highly for their beauty in the stories they hail from, yeah, I see why she treated people the way she did in the past. It had to have been rough on her. I was really looking forward to reading more about what she plans to do after the last TPB, but here there was really not much of anything advanced in regards to herstory, just a little bait to keep us all intrigued. It's good but I'd have liked to have learned more.<br /><br />One of my favorite parts in this TPB would have to be Rose Red becoming a paladin of hope. I really loved how Willingham used the stories of The Little Match Girl, the Goose Girl (The False Bride), and Santa Claus. I'm really impressed that he picked such good characters for the agents of hope. I guess I should point out that it does bug me a little that The False Bride made it sound as if in her story that her punishment (being put in a barrel with iron nails in it and then having the barrel rolled down the cobbles, killing her) was done just as a regular punishment... but in the actual story she was asked what punishment was deserving for what she did to the true bride. She said that such a person deserved nothing less than being killed in that manner. So she set HER OWN punishment. I thought if Bill Willingham had her say that part it would have added a greater level to her being the Spirit of Hope for Revenge.<br /><br />Other than that plotline, we get a bit more side stories involving the lustful Porky Pine the porcupine who is horny for human women. I'm pretty sure he showed up in some other arc but it's interesting to see how he gained the unusual fetish he has. Also an interesting side story involving a powerful sorcerer Abra Kadabara who has been the unspoken reason the Adversary's forces hadn't struck in Fabletown (until he is killed by Dorothy in a tie-in to the 2nd Cinderella miniseries). Fascinating stuff! Also there's a pretty fantastic backstory of the turtle with the cup on its shell from the background of the Rose Red trade.<br /><br />Suspense seems to be the theme with this volume. As other reviewers have pointed out Bill Willingham has really been setting many new and interesting twists in the Fables universe. Like a lot of the other reviewers on here, I do also wish that he would give some stories proper resolution before doing so many, but having been a Fables fan since I was 13 (way back when the first trades came out) I can already tell that when he resolves each of these, he's going to make them amaze his readers.<br /><br />The art is fantastic in this trade by the way. As always Mark Buckingham is phenomenal. He just keeps getting better and better, I adore the way he draws his characters and the expressiveness in his drawings has been even more pronounced now. The guest artists do a fantastic job as well. I always love when P. Craig Russell lens his art to a Fables story.<br /><br />All together I'd highly recommend this trade. It's a great continuation of current story arcs and has a lot of crucial suspense building moments that will leave the reader begging for more.

1451626657|5.0|Nobody needs me to say that \\"Catch 22\\" is one of the great novels of the 20th century.  It's incredibly powerful today, and it's hard to imagine the impact it must have had when it was published more than 50 years ago.<br /><br />The book strips away any pretense about the glory, intelligence, or fairness of war. It shows the cynical, base motives of the most human of actions: violence. It highlights the absurdities --- the utter contradictions --- that each of us face every day in our lives.<br /><br />The thing that struck me most about \\"Catch 22\\" on what's probably my fourth reading is that it's so densely packed.  There are entire paragraphs that you need to read again and again because they have a remarkable perspective about the human condition.  But then you struggle to remember them 10 pages later because of the new ideas that Heller has raised.  One technique that Heller has mastered to create this density (for all I know, he invented it) is absurdist statements:  \\"The Texas turned out to be good natured, generous and likeable. In three days, no one could stand him.\\"  There are hundreds of these passages in the book.<br /><br />Heller also uses rare adjectives and adverbs throughout the text, often paired with a more familiar word. Again this adds to the density of the reading, even as you turn to an online dictionary for a definition.<br /><br />The book is also memorably grueseome and sexual, sometimes together. The bomber pilots lead a warped life, and so even their leisure is warped.  They're whoring and cheating as much as possible, usually out in the open because they simply don't live in a just society any more.  As an old, starving Italian woman explains to Yossarian near the end of the book, when he asks her why Military Police destroyed her whorehouse: \\"Catch 22 says they have a right to do anything that we can't stop them from doing.\\"<br /><br />Just when you think you're going to like someone or that the book is going to be fun, Heller slams youwith a wounded or dead pilot or a child prostitute.  It never stops.<br /><br />Basically, you become like Yossarian, the protagonist, in that you want to just scream at the top of your lungs that everyone and everything is crazy, and that we need a new crop of adults to come in and change the situation.  Yet, because it's war and human nature, it will never happen.

B001C4AFOY|4.0|This is a fine film that concentrates on--as indicated in the title--the early years of Queen Victoria's reign.  Emily Blunt is lovely as the youthful Victoria. Previously, I had only seen Blunt in The Devil Wears Prada; while Blunt's role in Prada was not a particularly demanding one, I thought she still showed a great deal of promise as an actress. As it turns out, I was not wrong.<br /><br />Blunt is convincing in her portrayal of the youthful Victoria who makes her fair share of mistakes as she attempts to balance the demands of a monarch, but also displays early glimmers of the steely certitude that characterized Victoria's long, historic reign. While it goes without saying that Blunt is considerably easier on the eyes than the good queen, Blunt definitely does not skate by on her looks.  On the contrary, Blunt's Victoria is a multi-dimensioned character that is plagued by her own self-doubt, unscrupulous advisors, as well as the giddiness and naivet of her youth.<br /><br />Rupert Friend is excellent as the young Prince Albert who is under as much pressure from the house of Saxe-Coburg to marry Queen Victoria as Victoria is to simply marry anyone from the short list of worthy candidates.  The chemistry between Friend and Blunt is interesting and the strong bond that the two forge forms the rock-solid foundation for their future marriage together. Friend is physically suited for the role of Albert and the boyish charm that he brings to the role is as irresistible to the audience as it proves to be for Victoria.<br /><br />The costuming in the movie is simply stunning, as is the light and set direction.  One particular standout scene is an intimate candle-lit moment shared by Victoria and Albert as they play chess together.  Albert coyly, but intelligently, flirts with Victoria who glows in response to the handsome prince's attention; most of the shot is in shadow, except for Victoria and Albert who seem to give off their own illumination--it is sheer magic.<br /><br />I thoroughly enjoyed the film. While it is true certain liberties were taken with historic accuracy, I don't think the final outcome is particularly damaging to the overall integrity of the movie. It is a must-see for fans of period movies, the English monarchy, or even those who enjoy a charming love story.

B008GOAFBQ|3.0|There are alot of negative reviews for this disc which was rumoured to be a double disc event. For a 35th Anniversary reissue, this is not at all worthy of that moniker. It is a disappointing revist to say the least. Bob Ezrin approached KISS about this remix because he wanted to do something for its 35th. But the thing about Ezrin's recording is that so much of it was premixed during the making of it and many of the tracks were recorded \\"wet\\" (meaning that there was no way to EVER alter its effects, etc). So, from the get-go, this project had many limitations, as he notes in the cd booklet. There are differences, however.  For examples, an omitted background vocal from the archived tape of \\"Detroit Rock City\\" makes its debut along with an added effect to the end of the last verse. Also, Ace's stinging feedback intro to \\"King...\\" isn't as prominent. The piano is more upfront in \\"Great Expectations\\" . And there are other such differences throughout. Then there is \\"Sweet Pain\\" with a different solo (I, myself, was hoping that the different solo was going to be an Ace Frehley outtake) and louder harmonized gang vocals folowing the guitar part. Overall, the mix is more present. The piano agumentations on some songs is not as noticable (eg, Detroit...) and in the process it sounds more \\"band\\" like because it sounds more like just the four guys. The opposite approach could have produced a KISS Symphony version . The drum sound (as noticed in the beginning of \\"Shout...\\") is tighter, with less air.<br /><br />With all of the differences, however, this release is \\"nothing new\\" and the lack of fanfare is most distressing. Diehard fans will notice the differences but those who are just coming to the band will not even be able to appreciate them. So, for a 35th Anniversary, this really is targeted at diehards. If this present disc had been accompanied with a second disc of demos and outtakes, then there would hardly have been any negative reviews. The Remix is great for what it is. The packaging is good (although they SHOULD HAVE altered the color of the back to match the front artwork). The black disc even has a faux record label. There is so much that is good about this issue. But the complaints are valid. The bonus track is the original solo to \\"Sweet Pain\\"? THAT doesn't qualify for  an Anniversary acknowledgement. KISS has just rejoined Universal Music Group. They have nearly all of KISS' recorded stuff. There is plenty that they could have included. A live DVD or cd would have been SOMETHING. A stripped bonus disc would have been SOMETHING. A symphony remix as a bonus disc would have been SOMETHING. But what they have issued--great as it may be sonically--is unremarkable in value. I have bought this album in several formats numerous times. I don't regret buying this version, either. BUT all of it was unnecessary. Want to revisit something? How about a remix and remaster of Hot In The Shade? Some of the 80s percussive things on that disc, particularly \\"Read My Body\\" need an exit. How about a guitar-dominant remix of Unmasked? What if you release a version of The Elder with Ace's guitar parts present? There's a lot that could be remixed and remastered in the KISS World. BUT--and it's a big one--do it with determination. I'm not knocking Ezrin's remix. The disapointment is not with this issue's sonics. It's with its packaging, what it does not do because of what it does not include.<br /><br />Bottom line is that completists and die hards will buy it. New fans won't appreciate the differences. 1451627289|1.0|What a horrible book. I am amazed at all the positive reviews on here and elsewhere, sorry guys. I don't want to write about everything that was horrible, so I'll stick to a couple big issues: the beginning and the ending. Although it did a good job of producing some touching moments, the premise on which the book is based lacks any believability. The only thing that kept me reading the book was that I bought it before a 4 1/2 hour plane flight so I was several hundred pages in by the time we landed and I wanted to see if Mr. King really ended it how I thought he would. Here is why I thought the premise was ridiculous. The protagonist is 35, a generation X individual. I'm the same age, so let me tell you there is zero chance a normal gen X person goes back in time, giving up 5 years of his or her life, to stop the Kennedy assassination. One reason for that is that it's an event that has very little personal relevance to most of us because we didn't experience it. It's an immense event to those who lived through it, I completely respect the enormity of it and have empathy for the pain it caused all those affected. However you would have to be some twisted angst-ridden gen X-er who hated the present world in order to want to go back and change everything, and just having one alcoholic cheating ex-wife as a negative life experience does not get you to that point. Sorry. I can't think of one person I have ever met who would ever consider undertaking this ridiculous mission. Seriously, would you do it? I know it's a fantasy story, but character actions within a fantastical setting still have to make sense. As a side note, Mr. King's personal voice came through too loud and clear throughout this first-person narrative and jarred glaringly with the protagonists supposed age and experience. It was obviously the voice of a 65 year old, not a 35 year old.<br /><br />The ending was also painfully obvious and a lonnnnnnnnng time coming. Somehow the protagonist endures several years in the past and never seriously considers that the future outcome of saving Kennedy could be negative, despite the fact that outcomes may not turn out rosy as evidenced by experiences with the shooting victim and the Dunning family. He just seems to blindly go along with the idea propagated by the cancer-ridden diner proprietor that if Kennedy is saved, the world will be a better place. Any normal person in the protagonist's place would seriously consider a negative outcome however. Why didn't King have Jake ponder this issue at length? I believed when reading that it was because he wanted the reader to go along with the premise blindly as well so he or she would miss the parallel that changing the past leads to negative outcomes, and so it would in the Kennedy situation only in a really big way. So not too far in this book it was obvious that there were hundreds of pages of inane fiction that were going to get erased as a result of the protagonist popping up in a post-apocalyptic 2011 and then having to go back for a reset and allow the Kennedy assassination to take place after all. This is equivalent to the dream clich where a protagonist wakes up from a long sleep and realizes that whole eye-popping adventure they'd had never really happened. 11/22/63 has a ridiculous premise and a cliche and obvious ending.

B0070NSPCU|5.0|I think that I first found this book from some reddit /r/fantasy book recommendations, and when I checked the Amazon page and saw the horrible cover, I almost didn't check it out. However, since the book had so many good reviews and it was only 3 dollars I bought it, but didn't read it for a couple months. Now however, I'm kind of sad that I didn't read it earlier and that I only paid 3 dollars for this beast of a book. After the introduction, the book immediately grabbed me and there were multiple times where I would just read for hours, unable to put my Kindle down. The flavor of this novel is similar to the books from Patrick Rothfuss and Robin Hobb, but he doesn't copy anything from them other than just being similarly awesome. This novel spans many years, I'd say 10-20, and most of the conflicts and questions are resolved by the end, with the only ones remaining setting up a perfect starting place for the sequel. There aren't too many problems with this book, and the ones that are present could probably be fixed for when Anthony Ryan has his big publisher debut in 2013. These problems are mainly some grammar and spelling mistakes (most of them aren't too glaring), some confusion that stems from the narrative jumping around in time, possible problems with the perspective, and the voice of the character possibly being too aged starting off when he's 11 years old.<br /><br />For the world building, I like it, it's relatively similar to a medieval version of our world, with versions of European, African, Asian, and Middle-Eastern cultures/peoples. However, I think that this world is much more cramped, causing a lot of fighting. This world's magic relies a little on hand-waving, but I definitely like the style of it, where it's very much in the background and then it becomes more involved as the story moves forward, almost similar to A Song of Ice and Fire. I also like how the powers resemble innate gifts, kind of similar to the knacks in the Alvin Maker series. Therole of Religion is also very interesting in this book. The main character and his country's Faith is almost a combination of Atheism and Spiritualism, where they believe God is a Lie, but when you die there is an afterlife where your hard work will be rewarded and the passed ancestors are always watching down on you. The Faith is an interesting idea, but even more intriguing is how the religion is still the cause or excuse of many of the conflicts, where they try to convert the Deniers and teach them that their religion and god is a lie. I really liked this, and I also liked how there are 6 (or 7) different orders in their faith that rule it, with the 6th being the one the main character Vaelin al Sorna is given to at the age of 11. Their order is an advanced military service, where the brothers are violently trained to be a type of Warrior Monk. There is a lot of depth in the parts of the book covering this, and I immensely enjoyed it. I especially liked the psychology of this brotherhood, especially the main group of 5 featured in most of the book. There are some very interesting looks at PTSD and the life of a child turned death-dealing soldier. I also really enjoyed how Vaelin killed without any hesitation, but felt regret at times (but didn't get whiny about it) especially when he knows the person he killed was innocent but he still had to defend himself. It's great too, how every other brother of the sixth order deal with the after-effects of war differently, it's just really realistic and something you don't generally see in Fantasy novels.<br /><br />While the story relies on a few cliched gimmicks, mainly someone in the present orating their life-story for a scribe/historian, the author masterfully uses it to push along the plot. However, there some annoying parts dealing with the perspective. The Scribe/Historian in the present is in first-person, and when it switches to the past it is in third-person, but there are multiple times where the tense is broken and present Vaelin offers some sort of insight as to how he would replay this moment multiple times in the future (or something like that). Also, there are some very interesting but slightly confusing moments where we find out that while the reader learns the truth of everything that happened, what Vaelin's actually orating to the scribe is a lie or an altered version to keep his secrets safe. I liked that, but at the same time it confused me a bit, so I assume that as the reader we are witnessing Vaelin's life story in his mind before he modifies it for the in-world audience. This could also probably explain how in the first part of the book when the main character is around 11 years old, that he seems so adult. That we are actually experiencing all of the events through the filter of the mind of a late-twenties? year old warrior. There are also some instances especially towards the end  of the book where parts of the story are left out to increase the tension and drama for the reader for when we actually learn all of what happened. Again, I really liked these parts, but I was confused at times with how the narrative jumped back and forth. Some of this could be fixed by just including some sort of \\"5 Years Ago\\" or \\"Present Time\\" header so the reader isn't wondering at all when/where the character is (especially if the reader has trouble remembering the odd names of different places). The great thing though, is that by the end of this book we are all caught up, so hopefully in the sequel there won't be a lot of jumping around. The only problem is that it will be very interesting to see how Ryan handles telling his story completely in the present, he won't have the crutch of a character orating the past to jump around to the interesting parts of the story.<br /><br />For the story, this is definitely where the book shined. I loved how all of the characters, while being perfect killing machines, are very flawed beings. They make mistakes, perform thankless acts ofheroism, and are reviled for horrible acts they or their forebears committed. There is a lot we don't know about all of the main characters, and when the characters do let their secrets be known, they were all the more important. There are many coincidences and unbelievable moments in the story, but they are almost always very entertaining, and these can mostly be explained either by Vaelin's gift or people behind the scenes trying to unsuccessfully get rid of him. The characters are very complex, and I enjoyed all of the time I spent with them. The first half of the book or so where Vaelin and his Brothers are being trained and going through the trials are probably the best parts of the book. I love the things that come after, but the pace could be a little jarring at times, I felt like it was rushing to the climax. Still, all of the plot twists and different political and religious schemes are very interesting, even when they all fail miserably. Vaelin al Sorna is really the ultimate badass in this book, and all of the action involving him was incredibly visceral and entertaining. He's almost too powerful and talented, but there are multiple times where he's brought down by extenuating circumstances which increases the drama. I don't want to go too in depth with the story to avoid spoilers, but I will say that I loved all of it. The only thing is that with the ultimate ending, even though it answers a lot of questions, I found it lacking in a little power. With what happens, I think that the author was going for something really emotional, but I felt much more emotion (even a few tears) at other parts of the book, and I for some reason wasn't too sad or upset with the end. Still, I was left wishing that I had the sequel to read immediately, so I could see what happened with all of the other characters in the book and how they will react to seeing Vaelin 5 years later. I strongly hope that being picked up by a publisher doesn't delay his next book. :)<br /><br />Ultimately, I loved this book and I think that Anthony Ryan has found a place in my list of favorite authors. He's also gained a life-time fan in me, and I would absolutely recommend this book to anyone, especially if they're a fan of epic fantasy.

Twilight is not even in the same league as this movie and will fade away over time. What makes this movie so great is the story as well as the great acting. This movie is destined to become a CLASSIC in the mold of Fahrenheit 451, 1984 and other 'totalitarian state\\" movies/books. The story is eerily like where the world actually appears headed...where the elite 1% have 99% of the wealth and everyone else shares the remaining 1% of the wealth. And hence, the depiction of the decadence of the rich and their disdain for \\"the masses\\" really hits home and builds up our emotional \\"involvement\\" in the movie. Clearly, the author was shooting to sensitive us to the dangers of where we are headed. Anyway, the movie plot/story really feeds on this \\"us vs them\\" dynamic and does so brilliantly. What could be worse than the 1% demanding a \\"tribute\\" in the form of taking our children to die in a gory spectacle for their evil pleasure? The acting in this movie is superb by both the veterans and new-comers. The two main protagonists are both very appealing and you really want them to pull through. Donald Sutherland as the \\"king\\" of the 1% plays his role to evil perfection. And Woody....sleeper for best supporting actor? Too many great performance to list here. The cinematography is excellent and the scenes of battle/conflict are grim and realistic. And the feelings of young adults faced with being thrust into such a situation are played well. And lastly, the actress who plays Katniss is so \\"hot\\" that my new LCD TV almost melted. Another actress in this role and the movie would be still be good. But Jennifer puts this movie over the top...perfect casting. I have never been so wrong abouta movie going in relative to the hype and what I expected to see. 11 out of 10!!!

B005LAIGL4|4.0|Mid to late August has perennially been a weak time for new theatrical releases. When I was growing up, it was always the dumping grounds for expected hits that studios shuffled away in full acknowledgment that they weren't the films they were supposed to be (hello, \\"Superman IV: The Quest For Peace\\"). These days, some minor hits have popped up in the waning days of August, and this year has produced another sleeper: screenwriter David Koepp's exciting PREMIUM RUSH.<br /><br />A tidy, edge-of-your-seat thriller that recalls Kevin Bacon's `80s misfire \\"Quicksilver\\" in premise only, \\"Premium Rush\\" likewise follows a NYC bike messenger - here superbly portrayed by Joseph Gordon-Levitt - who's been charged with delivering a parcel to Chinatown...one that NYPD cop Michael Shannon is highly interested in. Trying to stay one step ahead of his pursuer, Gordon-Levitt darts in and out of midtown traffic, trying to figure out what he's carrying and help out a college acquaintance - a young Chinese woman - whose life seems to be riding on the outcome of his delivery.<br /><br />I've long criticized some of Koepp's scripts for high profile summer movies (particularly his efforts for Steven Spielberg, including the underwhelming \\"War of the Worlds\\" and\\"The Lost World: Jurassic Park\\"), but \\"Premium Rush\\" - written with John Kamps - ranks as one of his tightest, and most satisfying, screenplays, managing to mix a fast-moving action film with a twisty, compelling story that packs more of an emotional punch than anticipated. The authentic locations add immeasurably to the film's many scenes of Gordon-Levitt and his fellow bikers being an inch or two away from certain disaster, maneuvering through a maze-like succession of traffic, pedestrians and lousy Big Apple drivers; Koepp the director, meanwhile, deftly mixes in flashbacks to tell a story that works just perfectly in the short, 90-minute time frame employed here.<br /><br />\\"Premium Rush\\" didn't make much noise in theaters thispast weekend, and seemed to fly under the radar completely in terms of most viewers' pre-release awareness (I hadn't even heard of the film until it opened Friday). That's unfortunate, because it's the complete opposite of the bloated, overlong $200 million typical blockbuster most studios are interested in these days. If you're looking for a short but sweet, fresh Hollywood movie, \\"Premium Rush\\" puts a splendid cap on the (surprisingly good) summer movie season of 2012.

B00007FOMP|5.0|This record came out when I was in high school, and I remember buying it with babysitting money.  I was a caucasion girl living in a mostly caucasion neighborhood, with 4 siblings that simply LOVED all things Motown.  The Supremes reigned supreme with my oldest sister, and all of us had a fondness for The Temptations.  We had a split level home with steps that entered into our living room, and the stairway had floor to ceiling mirrors.  (Supposedly a trick to make the room appear larger.) Anyway, we would push the furniture out of the way, line up, all five of us facing the mirror in dance position, and turn on The Temptations.  We would sing and dance in unison, just like the Temptations did.  Yes, for those few minutes we WERE The Temptations!  My point is, these folks spoke to me and my siblings!  Of course, not like Marvin Gaye's \\"What's Going On.\\"<br /><br />Marvin Gaye is the GREATEST singer that ever lived and it is evident on this album.  Motown employed some of the best musicians on the planet to work in their studios and with their artists and their releases were well played.  Top it off with some of the best production people and you have Motown's recipe for the success they earned and deserved over the years.  This album was released at a difficult time for our country.  People were uneasy with war, riots, poverty, and a loss of innocence  with assassinations of one president, one civil rights leader, and a presidential candidate. Motown's artists appealed to people of all races and they really didn't want to risk that by delving into anything perceived as political.  Marvin Gaye took them, and us, to a place we didn't really want to go.  Oh, boy, I was ready to go!  I have had this record in my collection since its release. I've worn out two LP's, broken one cassette, scratched one old CD and now own the Deluxe Remastered Version.  It still stands up today as one of the best albums ever made by anyone, and for me it will always remain one of my top tenof all time.<br /><br />Everyone should have this record in their collection.  It is as close to perfect as music gets with great, great, vocals, Mr. Gaye's as well as the backing vocalists, extraordinary backing musicians, exquisite production, and lyrics that are relevant and thought provoking.  It was like Mr. Gaye was tapping you gently on the shoulder and saying, hey, think about this for a minute will you?  Oh, and it contains one of the greatest bass lines ever recorded on the song \\"Inner City Blues.\\"<br /><br />I highly recommend this groundbreaking CD to everyone that has even a remote interest in music.  If you only own one CD, make it this one.

Stay true to these characters or you will end up ruining this great series. I *still* liked this episode, but it did feel like I had to suspend belief about how my favorite characters would act throughout this episode. Several times they seemed to stay in one place longer than I thought they would, ruminating about what their next move would be, instead of the quick thinking and action that was so characteristic of Season One.

0007477325|4.0|There is a dark subtext to this well-written entertaining book. Had this subtext been acknowledged up front and pressed into action, I believe this book and its numerous real life experiments could have been a \\"teachable moment.\\" However, I find it rather incredible that this pregnant subtext was apparently overlooked altogether?<br /><br />The subtext is this: Once there was a time in the U.S. when there was something loosely referred to as a \\"culture of honesty.\\" It was not just an abstraction in the back of our collective imagination, but was what made up one of the unacknowledged pillars of our morality, a protective moral layers for our culture, as it were. It, and not the de facto Simple Model of Rational Crime (SMROC), introduced here, was THE moral cultural \\"given.\\" It was the moral code we all lived by. For no particular reason this protective film was everywhere, a cultural leitmotif that was one of the important byproducts of our mostly Christian upbringing. It was not (and was not seen as) an \\"add-on\\" or as an afterthought to the SMORC. It was the antidote to it - the cultural answer to the SMORC!<br /><br />We all knew that the SMORC existed, that it was the temptation \\"out there,\\" waiting to be plucked like the low hanging apple on the tree in the Garden of Eden. However, then (as opposed to now) being honest, resisting temptation, maintaining ones self-respect was the norm and not the SMORC. Being honest rather than -- \\"playing the angles, finding more moral wiggle room, sliding through the moral crevices, dancing on the pinhead full of moral angels, triangulating, compromising with evil, gaming or rigging the system in our favor, running a short or long con, and getting paid by any means necessary -- was the rule and not the exception. Unlike now, being privately honest was what was wired into our nervous systems.<br /><br />The price for making (and accepting) a rational calculation against the chances of being caught doing something wrong (i.e., apact with our inner devil) was infinitely costly, psychologically, socially and morally. Certainly there were many who took the chance anyway. But it is not an exaggeration to say that then the norm was that we were first morally accountable to ourselves and then afterwards to society. This embedded moral cultural strategy changed the moral calculus in favor of honesty for the entire culture in ways that we can no longer even account for. But something has changed, and rather drastically.<br /><br />I can remember when I first learned the lesson that this was the irreducible component of our moral cultural code. It was through a High School friend. Kenneth Caudle (Keno). We grew up in an academically very competitive segregated High School in Pine Bluff, Arkansas. At the end of our Junior year, fearing that Physics (under Mr. Bankston) might trip us up in the coming year, we concocted a scheme to order the Physics textbook from the publishers over the summer, and thereby get a jump on the competition, while at the same time allaying our fears about Physics. This devious plan worked out better than we had anticipated, as the publishers not only sent the textbooks, but also a teacher's guide with all of the problems in the text worked out in detail!<br /><br />I was ecstatic but Keno, my friend was not. Late one weekend night as I had finished escorting a date home on Keno's side of town, I saw a light on in his bedroom. I knocked on the door to see why he would be up so late on the weekend during the summer. Was I ever surprised to see that he was busy toiling over Physics problems? Naively I said, why are you worrying about Physics when we already have all the problems worked out for us? Then came the moral bolt of lightening from my friend that came as a revelation to me at the time, one that since that night I have never forgotten. He said: \\"You don't get it do you? We can't look at those answers before solving the problems ourselves because then we would only bestealing from ourselves?\\"<br /><br />The rest of the way home I had time to think about my friend's very sober message. He was right. By ordering the textbooks, we had only set a moral trap for ourselves: If we looked at the answers, we would only be cheating ourselves. We would not be learning Physics, but we would be learning how to cheat.<br /><br />As a result, we redoubled our efforts to work the problems without looking at the answers, even collaborating on the most difficult problems over the telephone, but never looking at the answers prior to exhausting all efforts to solve those problems. At least that was true in Keno's case. I will now confess that on a few occasions I did look at the answers. And I guess that is why Keno ended up being a Physicist and me a Political Scientist. The guy we were competing against, Clifton Roaf is now a respected Dentist in our hometown. All three of us ended up with A's in the Physics course. However, Keno and I earned our A's with an edge; Cliff apparently did not need an edge: Game, set, match to Roaf.<br /><br />What has changed is the context of our morality. Then honesty was the norm; dishonesty was made to be infinitely psychologically costly. No penalty that society could mete out would equal that one had to endure privately, within himself. Psychological guilt was part-and-parcel to our being (and seeing ourselves) as upstanding citizens. Self-enforced honesty was the price of the ticket -- the price of being a respected (and more importantly, self respecting) participant in our collective culture of honesty. We imbibed it through osmosis, through the normal socialization of arriving at adulthood (as I did in the lesson with my friend). The rules were rehearsed through example, through biblical parables and through imaginary/mythical models of integrity and fortitude. Failing these, this inner code was enforced and reinforced thorough more direct methods such as punishments or being socially ostracized, etc.<br /><br />The implicit cultural strategy then was: Yes, there are and always will be those who will cut corners, play the odds and angles, triangulate, slide through the moral crevices, and \\"game\\" or try to \\"rig\\" the system in their own favor. However, these people will see themselves (and will be seen by others) as \\"less than full moral beings\\" and they (and everybody else) knew it. Their \\"way of being in the world\\" existed on a lower rung of the moral ladder, somewhere on the border between the \\"low\\" and the \\"minimal acceptable\\" life. The \\"low\\" life was never accepted as an \\"understood\\" or as a \\"given\\" part of the American culture until recently. But now, all of our businessmen and politicians can certifiably be classified as \\"low-lifes,\\" well under the moral water line. And these are our models?!<br /><br />In short, the moral norm once was that self-respect came before respect, and that self-respect demanded that one had to tend to his own moral garden before being admitted to the larger collective cultural garden. One could not walk proudly with his chest sticking out expecting the respect of his peers without first understanding how to resist the temptation of the SMORC.<br /><br />Yet, over the decades, like everything else in our culture, inhibitions against \\"honesty as a cultural project,\\" has slowly eroded the protective moral coating that was once a given. And I agree with this author, that America's business has served as the organic wheel that is speeding downhill into the moral abyss. There is no longer such a thing as making an honest buck; all bucks nowadays are made the easy way: that is to say, dishonestly.  All our morally-challenged politicians and even our preachers, now use the SMORC as their starting point. Four Stars

B00005Y46O|5.0|I mentioned this once before in a review, but it's more relevant now: I had a fellow psychology tutor in college who mentioned that one day he was in a coffee house that played what seemed like an incessant sonic barrage of industrial heavy metal music, &quot;Then (he said), out of the blue they played some old BAD CO. and it sounded so good.&quot; Kind of like stepping from the heart of a big city into a beautiful forest. 140271453X|4.0|Some 560+ pages in length, encompassing 12 adventures and 11 memoirs, this is a fantastic book. The hardback version from Sterling Classic has a nice dust cover and a sash built into the spine to use as a book mark.<br /><br />Conan-Doyle is a master with the pen. His command of the language is both quaint, witty and somewhat verbose. It's perfect given the backdrop of Victorian England.  London of the time would have us experiencing Hansom cabs clippety-clopping along cobble stoned streets awash with the glow of gas lamps. Conan-Doyle makes the smell of the air and the noise of the times leap out the text and put the the reader right at the scene.<br /><br />Dr. Watson serves as both narrator and \\"straight man\\" as he recounts the adventures. Watson is the character Holmes uses to bounce his ideas off and, in turn, Watson asks the questions that we, the reader, would ask. It's great stuff. Holmes is usually sought after by people who find themselves in circumstances baffling to both themselves and the police. Holmes with his razor sharp focus and other-worldly insight muses and wrestles with the minutia of the case until he reaches a plausible explanation. Watson accompanies Holmes on his adventures and serves as both narrator and witness. I'm a huge fan of this work and, after reading, I'd assume most will be also.<br /><br />The great thing about short story compilations is that break points are plentiful as  each adventure averages about 25 to 30 pages each. I found myself enjoying one or two stories at a time then putting the book down whilst I read another novel or text. Upon picking it back up, there was no need to read back a page or two to recapture the thread. So is the strength of short story compilations.<br /><br />If quaint, witty, Victorian England type text is appealing, you might want to pick this book up. If you want to improve your vocabulary, read a classic, or just drift away in wondrous prose, pick this one up.<br /><br />Excellent work.

0060825456|4.0|It seems that this volume would work really well as a first graphic novel for a younger reader. You start with Gaiman's story and beautiful, creepy prose. About a quarter of the way through, I finally had a solid grasp on the different personalities. The entire book is told in a series of letters over the course of several months.<br /><br />England is healing in the aftermath of World War II and Juliet Ashton, a famed author, is looking for something to write about. Inspiration literally knocks on her door in the form of a letter from Dawson Adams, a pig farmer on Guernsey island who happened upon her name in a used book. This initiates an enchanting correspondence between Juliet and the members of the Guernsey Literary and Potato Peel Pie Society which was formed somewhat accidentally during German Occupation. Each letter shares a story- sometimes tragic, sometimes comical- demonstrating the everyday acts of bravery and defiance that shaped a difficult time. As Juliet grows closer to these islanders and the memories they have shared, she herself becomes a part of their rich history.<br /><br />Shaffer presents the story of this small corner of the world with amazing characterization. The people of Guernsey- their habits, their stories, and their love of literature- completely enamored me. To be honest, I didn't enjoy so much the banter between Juliet and her London friends or editors. Their personal conversations seemed more frivolous than the stories of the Guernsey residents. The real magic of the book lies in the kind and quirky natures of these resilient inhabitants. This book is wonderful both as historical fiction and as testament to the power of a book club.
