B002I0J5FG|3.0|I never played these games before this collection came out, but I was always interested in them because of the praise they seem to get from the gamer community. Having played the games now, I can say that the settings, environments and ambiance are amazing and  the minimalistic stories presented in both are pretty interesting, considering how little exposition there is. The graphics look really good in this HD remake, Shadow of the Colossus looks particularly impressive, I dare to say it could even pass as a low end PS3 game.<br /><br />----------------------<br />Shadow of the Colossus<br />----------------------<br />I played this game first because it seemed like the more entertaining of the two games (I mean, just look at the cover!). Well, I was wrong. I don't know when the fun is supposed to begin. I can count on less than one hand the number of colossus battles I actually enjoyed (there are 16 total). Most of the time, I was just relieved that they were FINALLY over. Since defeating colossi is all you do in this game, that's kind of a problem. If you expect a God of War-esque boss battle that requires timing, precision, or intense manuevering, you'll be severely disappointed. Instead, you will be battling with clunky controls, iffy platforming, an inept camera, and, every now and then, unintuitive puzzle mechanics.<br /><br />I think I could accurately describe 1/3 or more of my time playing this game as just waiting. Waiting for my horse to get me to the location of the colossus, waiting for him to expose his weakness, sometimes waiting for the god character to give me a hint at what to do, waiting for the Colossus to stop trying to shake me off, waiting while I swam across some unnecessarily large lake, waiting 5 seconds for my character to get up after getting knocked down (literally, I counted). There was one Colossus, #11, that knocked me down repeatedly until I died, it never let me get back up, so I had fun waiting the 2 or 3 minutes it took for it tofinally kill me because I had given up trying to escape. Oh and if you fall off a Colossus, have fun waiting until you have the chance to get back on. There aren't many moments in my gaming history that I've felt more frustrated then when I fell off some of the taller Colossi only to have to go through the whole routine again. If you are an impatient person, stay far, far away from this game.<br /><br />There are only 2 or 3 Colossi that have any real chance of killing you, which definitely detracts from any possible feeling of excitement. Most of them don't do anything dangerous in particular and they move so slowly the only way you could get hurt is if you weren't paying any attention. And I feel the need to mention: There are only 2 battles in which you use your horse. Not surprisingly, these are by far the most exciting. I find it odd they didn't incorporate the horse you're on for significant portions of the game into more battle sequences. Talk about wasted potential.<br /><br />SotC has an expansive, beautiful environment you can choose to explore if you'd like, but it is utterly lifeless so there's not much incentive to go anywhere but to the next Colossus.<br /><br />---<br />Ico<br />---<br />This may surprise some, but I had a much more enjoyable experience with this game than with SotC, but it certainly isn't a perfect game. For one, combat in this game is atrocious.You get a weapon upgrade a few hours into the game which makes fights much shorter (thank god), but I still avoided combat like the plague. And to make matters worse, when you get hit you get knocked down for a few seconds just like in SotC. I imagine this was an intended gameplay element to increase the drama of your battles against the shadows while defending Yorda, but it only serves to increase how much I hate these stupid encounters. Taking control away from the player for such lengths of time is never, ever enjoyable.<br /><br />The camera is also pretty messy. It really isn't too big of an issue until one of the shadows grabs Yorda and starts to fly away. It's tough to figure out where they're going a lot of times and the jerking around of the camera angle only adds to my dislike of the combat. Otherwise, the camera is acceptable. I fell to my death a handful of times because I couldn't tell if I could make a jump but nothing truly gamebreaking.<br /><br />Combat isn't the main focus of this game though, so thankfully the puzzle/platforming is actually done pretty well throughout. For the most part, the puzzles are intuitive though there is one section I had to look at a walkthrough for. Turns out, I had the right solution, but the puzzle required a perfectly timed jump. I thought I was doing everything in my power to do this puzzle correctly; I must have attempted this jump dozens of times and I was fully convinced I must have missed something. I spent the next hour backtracking and looking for a solution before caving and reluctantly looking at a guide. Well I'm glad I did because I don't know if I would have kept trying that jump otherwise.<br /><br />There's pretty much no music in this game. You can play this game with the TV muted and have a nearly identical experience. I'm sure this was intended as an atmospheric device, and I'm sure some people might prefer it that way, but this is a pretty big knock on the game as far as I'm concerned.<br /><br />----------<br />Conclusion<br />----------<br />In the end, these games, while sharing similar aesthetics and story-worlds, are completely different in how they play out. Overall, I think Ico is the better of the two games by far. My biggest gripe with SotC is that I feel like I'm not doing anything a lot of the time, just waiting around. Exploring the castle in Ico actually serves as a means to an end; you need to figure out where to go. Ico does a much better job of giving the player the feeling of making progress. This feeling is aided by the fact that you will see parts of the castle you'vealready passed through as you advance, making you think \\"hey I remember this place.\\" Except now you're seeing it from a different angle, forcing you to realize how far you've come. The only sense of progress I felt in SotC was seeing my colossus kill count increase. The game lacks a cohesiveness to tie it all together. This lack of focus is the reason the game seems to drag on and doesn't feel rewarding in my opinion. There is no story explanation in SotC until literally the end of the game, you get no weapon upgrades or new moves to use (not in your first playthrough at least), and the world is empty and lifeless. Right when you kill one colossus you're immediately on to go kill the next which makes the game feel like a never ending MMO fetch quest. SotC just comes off feeling disjointed and like a series of side-quests compared to Ico's very focused and linear journey. When I look back at SotC, I remember it in separate bits and pieces; my recollection of Ico is all the puzzles and environments blending together seamlessly into one unique experience.<br /><br />I honestly have very few positive things to say about Shadow of the Colossus. It isn't necessarily an awful game, it's just not fun. I'm not sure how the developers managed to make battles with huge towering giants boring and forgettable, but they did. I can't recommend this collection for SotC, but Ico is a worthy game that holds up pretty well, even though it was made 4 years earlier (yikes).<br /><br />I do suggest you play this collection, despite all the negative things I've mentioned. They are both pretty short; Ico lasts from 5-6 hours, SotC is about 7-8. I'm sure if I played these games back in 2001 and 2005 I would have liked them more, but I can't rate games based on what ifs.

1451627289|5.0|The time travel device of this book works because King is interested not in the Sci-Fi elements, but in the emotional and philosophical issues raised by the idea of time travel.  Thus, there is little explanation of how the time travel device works -- it's just there, as a &#34;rabbit hole&#34;.  The reason we never get tired of reading about time travel is our desire to correct our mistakes; our desire to know whether there is a design in the universe - is life simply absurd and do we truly choose our own destinies; and the sneaking feeling of nostalgia that maybe things were better back in the day.  King thinks that while we can make a difference by our life choices, neither the past (nor the present, for that matter) really can be changed fundamentally.  All we can do is fall in love and make a difference in the immediate world around us.  As for the past -- in some ways it really was a lot better.  The food is better; the cars are better; the sense of freedom and control of our own destinies is stronger.  But the racism, tolerance for squalor, and environmental irresponsibility of the past can't be sustained.  In that sense,we can truly say we are making some progress.  The people are about the same, though King captures some of the real nastiness of 1963 Dallas and 1958 New England.  But even in these environments, truly strong, admirable, and unforgettable characters emerge.  On the downside, King does tend to the sentimental and some characters and conversations are real clunkers.  And while I'm satisfied with the conclusion, I think the alternative histories offered could have been a bit more subtle and compelling.  But the book is plain excellent and impossible to put down.  It will make a great movie -- and hopefully one is made.

Diesel reprises his role of Toretto, this time on the run from a high-octane federal law enforcement team led by Luke Hobbs, played by Dwayne Johnson.<br /><br />Complicating their situation further is Brazilian drug kingpin Hernan Reyes, portrayed by veteran actor Joaquim de Almeida of \\"Desperado\\" fame. Fast Five is chock full of tense moments, fast cars and shootouts. Adding a bit of levity to an otherwise hardcore action flick are the duo of reggaeton artists Don Omar and Tego Calderon, who return to the series with comedic relief in tow. Tyrese Gibson, Ludacris, Jordana Brewster, Sung Kang, and Matt Schulze also reprise their roles to offer a sense of familiarity to viewers.<br /><br />Fast Five's musical score is a mix of high-energy tracks with greater variety than other soundtracks from the franchise. The film's score doesn't feature as much American rap as earlier films, though it makes up for it with the inclusion of urban Portuguese-language songs from Brazil. International music ranges from Rio's rap to Brazilian Funk, plus the infectious \\"Danza Kuduro\\" by Puerto Rican reggaetonero Dom Omar, who returns to the film as the pessimistic Rico. Fast Five is the third film of the franchise that composer Brian Tyler contributes to, with songs ranging from cinematic to industrial rock.<br /><br />There are two elements of the film that stand out. The first being the too-good-to-be-true clash between Dom and federal strike team leader Luke Hobbs. The two reaching their inevitable on-screen collision hits with the force of a scud missile soaked in testosterone.<br /><br />The other element is the final car chase of the film. Gone are the intricate CGI shots of the mechanical workings of the cars involved. But what Fast Five loses in computer imaging, it makes up for with hardcore driving. The ultimate chase scene in the movie is far from what you've seen in other heist flicks, and is sure to please even the pickiest of action junkies.<br /><br />That's not to say that Fast Five is Oscar-quality, of course. To truly enjoy this film, you have accept in advance that this isn't Shawshank Redemption. While much of the cast have experience in film and television, the majority serve up average acting at best. I'm a huge fan of Vin Diesel's movies (Pitch Black FTW), but he looks all of his 44 years in this film, and his acting ability hasn't improved much. While he and Paul Walker both are well-suited for action flicks, don't expect profound performances from either of them. Diesel is given more of an opportunity to flex his acting chops in Fast Five, with it's emphasis on relationships and heartbreak, but I honestly wasn't too moved.<br /><br />Dwayne \\"The Rock\\" Johnson carries the greatest screen presence of the entire cast, giving off an air of intensity that resonates throughout every scene. But that being said, he wasn't given an opportunity to show off any real range of emotions in the film.<br /><br />Despite Fast Five having it's fair share of action and suspense, the film does drag on a bit longer than necessary with it's runtime of two-plus hours. While the film's conclusion is fairly clear, it's also given an open-ending that paves the way for the franchise's sixth effort, and the possible return of a significant character from the series.<br /><br />Overall, Fast Five is simply a fun watch, a guilty pleasure if you're a fan of heist flicks or action movies in general. If you're a motor head, you might be a bit let down at the abandonment of the franchise's original formula, though the change still make for a welcome spin on the series. Definitely get the Steelbook Blu-Ray to own one of the better movies in the series.><br />Overall, Fast Five is simply a fun watch, a guilty pleasure if you're a fan of heist flicks or action movies in general. 0374275637|5.0|I first encountered the work of Daniel Kahneman and his collaborator Amos Tversky when I was in graduate school.  A student of artificial intelligence, I was keenly interested in the question of how to design systems that can behave \\"rationally\\" despite their computational limits.  I was thinking about computer systems, but of course human beings are also systems that have limits on the amount of \\"computation\\"--or at least thinking--that they can do.  The adage of economists that rational behavior consists in doing the actions that do the most to further one's goals given one's beliefs simply didn't seem to make a lot of sense when considered from a computational perspective:  weighing one's options in light of one's goals and beliefs takes time, and if you try to weigh all your options, the world will change before you've finished.  As various people have said, human beings are not \\"frictionless deliberators.\\"<br /><br />Enter Kahneman and Tversky, whose work showed that people deviate in systematic ways from the standard model of rational thinking.  The key insight is that the deviations are systematic--and they're systematic in ways that accord with having finite reasoning capabilities.  It's not that people are irrational--Kahneman bristles at that description of his results.  Rather it's that the model of rational thinking developed by the economists may be fine as a normative model, but can't work as a descriptive one.<br /><br />Kahneman and Tversky collaborated on psychological studies of reasoning for more than a decade, writing hugely influential papers, including their 1974 Science article \\"Judgment under Uncertainty:  Heuristics and Biases,\\" which is amongst the most highly cited paper in economics, and which reproduced in full in \\"Thinking Fast and Slow.\\"  Tversky died in 1996 at the age of 59, but Kahneman went on to win the Nobel Prize in Economics for their joint work in 2002.  Indeed, \\"Thinking Fast and Slow\\" is a joy to read not only for the science it contains, but also, secondarily, for the touching accounts he provides of his long collaboration with Tversky.<br /><br />This is Kahneman's magnum opus:  an overarching review of 50 years of research that he conducted, first with Tversky, and subsequently with other leading social scientists.  It's remarkably accessible, even when he's describing somewhat arcane points of psychology.  Amongst the many cognitive biases he describes are these:<br /><br />* Seemingly little things that make it easier to process information--the size or darkness of the font in text, for example--have a powerful effect on the likelihood that you will believe or approve of the information.<br /><br />* We readily adopt a principle of WYSIATI--what you see is all there is.  We neglect the possibility that our conclusions may be biased by having only incomplete information.<br /><br />* We tend to apply a halo effect in assessing other people's character or capabilities:  we make specific assessments based on overall impression of them, and the errors that this induces are compounded by WYSIATI.<br /><br />* When we're trying to answer a challenging question, we often substitute an easier one, without even realizing it.<br /><br />* Our numeric judgments are biases by \\"anchoring effects\\" in which we are swayed by  sometimes irrelevant numbers that we encounter during our reasoning process.<br /><br />* We often assess the quality of an experience by what happens at the end of it.<br /><br />These are just six of the dozens of biases that Kahneman describes.  For each bias, he presents the sometimes astonishing psychological studies that support it.  For example, the last bias was uncovered in experiment with a group of patients who underwent colonoscopies in the early 1990s, when anesthesia was not well-administered for these procedures.  One group of patients had a few extra minutes added to the procedure at the end, during which they experienced a relatively reduced level of pain.The patients in that group felt that the experience was overall less unpleasant, even though they'd experienced all the pain of the other group plus some more!<br /><br />There's an overarching coherence to Kahenman's work:  he explains human decision-making and the cognitive biases it includes, in terms of two systems:  a quick-and-dirty Malcolm-Gladwell-like System 1, which, when needed, provides information and tentative conclusions to a more effortful, deliberative System 2.  However, while you might be tempted to think that the errors produced by the types of cognitive biases listed above are solely the result of System 1, that would be incorrect.  In fact, the connection between \\"accurate\\" reasoning and two systems is more complex, and even when humans rely on System 2, they are still not able to behave in the \\"fully rational\\" ways that would be specified by classical economics.  It takes some careful reading of Kahneman's book to sort this out, but on careful consideration one realizes that even with System 2 the economist's rationality can't be fully obtained.  How could it, by creatures with finite brains?
